fill="#848484" fill-rule="evenodd" d="M.74 14.24h2.964V4.695H.74zM3.797 2.041c0 .82-.604 1.483-1.599 1.483-.936 0-1.54-.664-1.54-1.483 0-.84.623-1.483 1.579-1.483s1.54.644 1.56 1.483M5.474 id="sidebarContent"><p class="sidebar_navTopLabel__xl_5k">コンテンツ</p><ol><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK sidebar_linkActive__wuL2e" rel="noopener" href="#article">Article</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" rel="noopener" href="#more-blogs">おすすめのブログ</a></li></ol></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0  hidden lg:flex" style="border-bottom:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">URLをコピー</button></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0 flex lg:hidden" style="border-bottom:none;border-top:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">URLをコピー</button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden lg:block" style="margin-bottom:-0px"></div><div class="" id="article"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><p>Zscaler’s ThreatLabz research team has been closely monitoring a campaign targeting users in South Korea.  This threat actor has been active for more than a year and continues to evolve its tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs); we believe with high confidence that the threat actor is associated with Lazarus Group, a sophisticated North Korean advanced persistent threat (APT) group.</p><p>In 2021, the main attack vector used by this threat actor was credential phishing attacks through emails, posing as Naver, the popular South Korean search engine and web portal. </p><p>In 2022, the same threat actor started spoofing various important entities in South Korea, including <strong>KRNIC </strong>(Korea Internet Information Center), Korean security vendors such as <strong>Ahnlab</strong>, cryptocurrency exchanges such as <strong>Binance, </strong>and others. Some details about this campaign were published <a href="https://blog.alyac.co.kr/4586"><u>in this Korean blog,</u></a> however they did not perform the threat attribution.</p><p>Even though the TTPs of this threat actor evolved over time, there were critical parts of their infrastructure that were reused, allowing ThreatLabz to correlate the attacks and do the threat attribution with a high-confidence level. Our research led us to the discovery of command-and-control (C2) domains even before they were used in active attacks by the threat actor. This proactive discovery of attacker infrastructure helps us in preempting the attacks.</p><p>In this blog, we will share the technical details of the attack chains, and will explain how we correlated this threat actor to Lazarus.</p><p>We would like to thank <strong>Dropbox </strong>for their quick action in taking down the malicious accounts used by the threat actor, and for also sharing valuable threat intelligence that helped us with threat attribution.<br/> </p><h2><strong>Attack chains</strong></h2><p>This threat actor has frequently updated its attack chains over the last two months. We identified three unique attack chains used by the threat actor to distribute the malware in emails:</p><p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Attack%20Flow.png" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Attack Flow" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><em>Figure 1: Attack flow</em></p><p> </p><h2><strong>Spear phishing emails distribution</strong></h2><p>During our analysis, we discovered that at least one of the IP addresses <strong>(222.112.127[.]9)</strong> used by the threat actor to log in to the attacker-controlled Dropbox accounts was also used to send spear phishing emails to the victims in South Korea.</p><p>Below are examples of two such emails that were sent from the IP address <strong>222.112.127[.]9</strong>. </p><p><strong>Note:</strong> This IP address is related to <strong>KT Corporation</strong>, a Korean telecom provider. Multiple IP addresses related to KT Corporation were abused by this threat actor during the current attack.</p><p><strong>Email #1</strong></p><p>In this email, a macro-based document was sent to the victim.</p><p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Email%201.png" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Email-1" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><em>Figure 2: Email sent to the victim</em></p><p>Figure 3 below shows that the decoy content of the document is related to <strong>Menlo Security</strong> company. This is consistent with other decoy contents used by the threat actor. For instance, in the document with MD5 hash: 1a536709554860fcc2c147374556205d, the decoy content used was related to <strong>Ahnlab </strong>- a Korea-based computer security company. This is done for the purpose of social engineering.</p><p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Decoy.png" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Decoy" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><em>Figure 3: Decoy content</em></p><p><strong>Email #2</strong></p><p>In this email, a password protected macro-based XLS file was sent to the victim. The password for the file was mentioned in the email body.</p><p>The theme of the file is related to cryptocurrency investments. This theme is consistent with other documents sent in this campaign as well. Lazarus Group is known to have a keen interest in attacking cryptocurrency users, asset managers, and companies.</p><p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Email%202.png" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Email-2" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><em>Figure 4: Email sent to the victim</em></p><p>Figure 5 below shows the sender&#x27;s IP address in the email headers as indicated by the X-Originating-IP field.</p><p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Email%20Header.png" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Email Header" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><em>Figure 5: Email header showing originating IP, Sender and Recipient</em></p><p> </p><h2><strong>Threat attribution</strong></h2><p>In order to perform the threat actor attribution, we did a correlation of the below data points.</p><p><strong>1.</strong> C2 IP addresses</p><p><strong>2.</strong> Attacker-controlled Dropbox accounts’ registrant email addresses</p><p><strong>3.</strong> C2 domains’ registrant email addresses</p><p><strong>4.</strong> Passive DNS data</p><p><strong>5.</strong> Sender&#x27;s email address in credential phishing attacks</p><p><strong>6.</strong> Sender&#x27;s IP address in credential phishing attacks </p><p><strong>Note:</strong> OSINT information related to the above data points was also used in correlation analysis.</p><p> </p><h3><strong># Correlating different attacks to same threat actor</strong></h3><p>As described in the network communication section later in the blog, the Stage-3 binary initially connects to an attacker-controlled Dropbox account to fetch a C2 domain which is used to perform further network communication.</p><p>In collaboration with Dropbox, we were able to discover the email addresses associated with the attacker-controlled Dropbox accounts used during this attack. One such email addresses was: <strong>peterstewart0326@gmail[.]com</strong></p><p>This same email address was recently mentioned in Prevailion&#x27;s <u>blog</u>. It was linked to several domains which were used during Naver-themed phishing activity.</p><p>Also, according to this<a href="https://blog.naver.com/PostView.naver?blogId=mrs_english&amp;logNo=222289029970"> <u>blog</u></a> from 2021, this same email address was also used to send Naver-themed credential phishing attack emails to users in South Korea.</p><p>Correlating the above data points, we can say with a high confidence level that the attack chains we have described in this blog are also related to the same threat actor.</p><p> </p><h3><strong># Attribution to Lazarus APT</strong></h3><p>According to the threat infrastructure mapping done in Prevailion <u>blog</u>, the IP address <strong>23.81.246[.]131</strong> belongs to one of the critical nodes used by the threat actor during Naver-themed phishing activity. One of the domains linked to this IP address was <strong>navercorpservice[.]com</strong>. If we check the passive DNS data for this domain, we find two other IP address resolutions: <strong>172.93.201[.]253</strong> in November 2021 and <strong>45.147.231[.]213</strong> in September 2021.  </p><p>The IP address <strong>172.93.201[.]253</strong> was recently used to host the domain - <strong>disneycareers[.]net</strong> which was attributed to Lazarus APT in Google TAG<a href="https://blog.google/threat-analysis-group/countering-threats-north-korea/"> <u>blog</u></a>. </p><p>Further, what caught our attention was the IP address <strong>45.147.231[.]213</strong>. This IP address was earlier used by North Korea-based APT threat actor. Recently, we also had a new domain resolution alert for this IP address as part of our C2 infrastructure tracking. If we pivot on the passive DNS data for this IP address, we can see that the domain: <strong>www.devguardmap[.]org</strong> was hosted on this IP address in Jan 2021 which was attributed to Lazarus APT as per this<a href="https://twitter.com/ESETresearch/status/1458438155149922312"> <u>tweet</u></a> from ESET and Google TAG<a href="https://blog.google/threat-analysis-group/update-campaign-targeting-security-researchers/"> <u>blog</u></a>.</p><p>Correlating all the above data points, we reached the conclusion that the attack-chains we discovered are related to Lazarus threat actor. To the best of our knowledge, at the time of writing, this threat actor attribution has not been publicly documented yet.</p><p> </p><h2><strong>Technical analysis</strong></h2><p>For the purpose of technical analysis we will consider the attack chain starting with a Compiled HTML file having MD5 210db61d1b11c1d233fd8a0645946074.</p><p> </p><h3><strong>[+] Stage 1: Compiled HTML file</strong></h3><p>The CHM file contains a malicious binary embedded inside it. At runtime, this will be dropped on the filesystem in the path: C:\\programdata\\chmtemp\\chmext.exe and executed.</p><p>The code responsible for extracting, dropping and executing the binary is present inside 1hh.html as shown below.</p><p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/CHM.png" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Compiled HTML" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><em>Figure 6: HTML code dropping and executing the binary</em></p><p> </p><h3><strong>[+] Stage 2: Dropper</strong></h3><p>The dropper on execution performs the following operations:</p><p><strong>1.</strong> Detects sleep patching to identify controlled execution environment such as Sandbox execution</p><p><strong>2.</strong> Checks the name of all the running processes and terminates if it finds a process running with the name <strong>&quot;v3l4sp.exe&quot;</strong>. This process name corresponds to the security software developed by Ahnlab (a popular and frequently used security vendor in South Korea).</p><p><strong>3.</strong> Creates file in the path <strong>&quot;C:\ProgramData\Intel\IntelRST.exe&quot;</strong></p><p><strong>4.</strong> XOR decodes the embedded PE from a hardcoded address</p><p><strong>5. </strong>Writes the decoded PE to the file created in Step-3</p><p><strong>6.</strong> Modifies PEB to masquerade itself as explorer.exe</p><p><strong>7. </strong>Executes IntelRST.exe</p><p><strong>8.</strong> Creates RUN registry entry for persistence</p><p><strong>Value:</strong> IntelCUI</p><p><strong>Data: </strong>C:\ProgramData\Intel\IntelRST.exe</p><p> </p><h3><strong>[+] Stage 3: Dropped binary</strong></h3><p>The file IntelRST.exe dropped by the Stage-2 dropper is an ASpacked binary. On execution it performs the following operations:</p><p><strong>1. </strong>Similar to the dropper binary it tries to detect sleep patching to identify controlled execution environment</p><p><strong>2. </strong>Collects machine information and stores using the specified format which is later exfiltrated and used as machine identifier.</p><p><strong>String format:</strong> </p><p>[decoded_string]_[username]_[volume_serial_number_post_8_bytes]</p><p><strong>decoded_string:</strong> (encoded string) ^ (key) [encoded_string_byte_offset%keySize]</p><p><strong>username:</strong> GetUserName()</p><p><strong>volume_serial _number:</strong> Using DeviceIoControl with IOCTL_STORAGE_QUERY_PROPERTY (0x2d1400)</p><p><strong>3.</strong> Checks name of all the running processes and terminates if there is some process running with the name &quot;v3l4sp.exe&quot; or &quot;AYAgent.aye&quot; or &quot;IntelRST.exe&quot;</p><p><strong>4.</strong> If running with administrator privileges then it executes a PowerShell command using cmd.exe to add WindowsDefender exclusion.</p><p><strong>PowerShell command:</strong> Powershell -Command Add-MpPreference -ExclusionPath &quot;C:\ProgramData\Intel\IntelRST.exe</p><p><strong>5.</strong>  Finally it starts the network communication</p><p> </p><h3><strong>[+] Network communication</strong></h3><p>The network communication occurs in the following sequence:</p><p><strong>1. </strong>Send a GET request to the URL &quot;https://dl.dropboxusercontent.com/s/k288s9tu2o53v41/zs_url.txt?dl=0&quot;. </p><p><strong>2. </strong>Query the file size and send another network request to read the file content.</p><p><strong>Note:</strong> The file content points to the C2 domain to be used for rest of the network communication.</p><p><strong>3.</strong> Using the extracted C2 domain, send a POST request to the path &quot;/post.php&quot; and exfiltrate collected user information.</p><p><strong>Exfiltrated user information format:</strong></p><p>uid={string_generated_in_Step-2_of_Stage-3_binary}&amp;avtype=%d&amp;majorv=%d&amp;minorv=%d</p><p><strong>4.</strong> Finally send a GET request to the path &quot;/{decoded_string_from_step-2_of_Stage-3_binary}/{formated_string_from_step-2_of_Stage-3_binary}/fecommand.acm&quot;</p><p><strong>Note:</strong> At the time of analysis we didn&#x27;t get any active response from the C2 server for the above network request.</p><p> </p><h2><strong>Zscaler Cloud Sandbox detection</strong></h2><p><strong># Document detection</strong></p><p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Document%20Detection.png" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Document - Sandbox Report" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><strong># Dropper detection</strong></p><p><img src="https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Binary%20Detection.png" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Dropper - Sandbox Report" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><br/> </p><p> </p><h2><strong>Indicators of compromise</strong></h2><p><strong>[+] path</strong></p><p>D:\Works\PC_2022\ACKS_2012\fengine\Release\fengine.pdb</p><p> </p><h2><strong>About us</strong></h2><p><a href="https://threatlabz.zscaler.com/cloud-insights/cloud-activity-dashboard"><u>Zscaler ThreatLabz</u></a> is a global threat research team with a mission to protect customers from advanced cyberthreats. Made up of more than 100 security experts with decades of experience in tracking threat actors, malware reverse engineering, behavior analytics, and data science, the team operates 24/7 to identify and prevent emerging threats using insights from 300 trillion daily signals from the Zscaler Zero Trust Exchange.</p><p>Since its inception, ThreatLabz has been tracking the evolution of emerging threat vectors, campaigns, and groups, contributing critical findings and insights on zero-day vulnerabilities, —including active IOCs and TTPs for threat actors, malware, and ransomware families, phishing campaigns, and more.</p><p>ThreatLabz supports industry information sharing and plays an integral role in the development of world-class security solutions at Zscaler. See <a href="/jp/blogs/security-research">the <u>latest ThreatLabz threat research</u></a> on the Zscaler blog.</p></div></div>

      <p><span>免責事項：このブログは、Zscalerが情報提供のみを目的として作成したものであり、「現状のまま」提供されています。記載された内容の正確性、完全性、信頼性については一切保証されません。Zscalerは、ブログ内の情報の誤りや欠如、またはその情報に基づいて行われるいかなる行為に関して一切の責任を負いません。また、ブログ内でリンクされているサードパーティーのWebサイトおよびリソースは、利便性のみを目的として提供されており、その内容や運用についても一切の責任を負いません。すべての内容は予告なく変更される場合があります。このブログにアクセスすることで、これらの条件に同意し、情報の確認および使用は自己責任で行うことを理解したものとみなされます。</span></p>

ThreatLabz research team has been closely monitoring a campaign targeting users in South Korea.  This threat actor has been active for more than a year and continues to evolve its tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs); we believe with high confidence that the threat actor is associated with Lazarus Group, a sophisticated North Korean advanced persistent threat (APT) group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn 2021, the main attack vector used by this threat actor was credential phishing attacks through emails, posing as Naver, the popular South Korean search engine and web portal. \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn 2022, the same threat actor started spoofing various important entities in South Korea, including \u003cstrong\u003eKRNIC \u003c/strong\u003e(Korea Internet Information Center), Korean security vendors such as \u003cstrong\u003eAhnlab\u003c/strong\u003e, cryptocurrency exchanges such as \u003cstrong\u003eBinance, \u003c/strong\u003eand others. Some details about this campaign were published \u003ca href=\"https://blog.alyac.co.kr/4586\"\u003e\u003cu\u003ein this Korean blog,\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e however they did not perform the threat attribution.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEven though the TTPs of this threat actor evolved over time, there were critical parts of their infrastructure that were reused, allowing ThreatLabz to correlate the attacks and do the threat attribution with a high-confidence level. Our research led us to the discovery of command-and-control (C2) domains even before they were used in active attacks by the threat actor. This proactive discovery of attacker infrastructure helps us in preempting the attacks.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn this blog, we will share the technical details of the attack chains, and will explain how we correlated this threat actor to Lazarus.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe would like to thank \u003cstrong\u003eDropbox \u003c/strong\u003efor their quick action in taking down the malicious accounts used by the threat actor, and for also sharing valuable threat intelligence that helped us with threat attribution.\u003cbr\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAttack chains\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis threat actor has frequently updated its attack chains over the last two months. We identified three unique attack chains used by the threat actor to distribute the malware in emails:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Attack%20Flow.png\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" data-entity-type=\"\" alt=\"Attack Flow\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 1: Attack flow\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSpear phishing emails distribution\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eDuring our analysis, we discovered that at least one of the IP addresses \u003cstrong\u003e(222.112.127[.]9)\u003c/strong\u003e used by the threat actor to log in to the attacker-controlled Dropbox accounts was also used to send spear phishing emails to the victims in South Korea.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBelow are examples of two such emails that were sent from the IP address \u003cstrong\u003e222.112.127[.]9\u003c/strong\u003e. \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNote:\u003c/strong\u003e This IP address is related to \u003cstrong\u003eKT Corporation\u003c/strong\u003e, a Korean telecom provider. Multiple IP addresses related to KT Corporation were abused by this threat actor during the current attack.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eEmail #1\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn this email, a macro-based document was sent to the victim.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Email%201.png\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" data-entity-type=\"\" alt=\"Email-1\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 2: Email sent to the victim\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 3 below shows that the decoy content of the document is related to \u003cstrong\u003eMenlo Security\u003c/strong\u003e company. This is consistent with other decoy contents used by the threat actor. For instance, in the document with MD5 hash: 1a536709554860fcc2c147374556205d, the decoy content used was related to \u003cstrong\u003eAhnlab \u003c/strong\u003e- a Korea-based computer security company. This is done for the purpose of social engineering.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Decoy.png\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" data-entity-type=\"\" alt=\"Decoy\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 3: Decoy content\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eEmail #2\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn this email, a password protected macro-based XLS file was sent to the victim. The password for the file was mentioned in the email body.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe theme of the file is related to cryptocurrency investments. This theme is consistent with other documents sent in this campaign as well. Lazarus Group is known to have a keen interest in attacking cryptocurrency users, asset managers, and companies.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Email%202.png\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" data-entity-type=\"\" alt=\"Email-2\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 4: Email sent to the victim\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 5 below shows the sender's IP address in the email headers as indicated by the X-Originating-IP field.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/Email%20Header.png\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" data-entity-type=\"\" alt=\"Email Header\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 5: Email header showing originating IP, Sender and Recipient\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eThreat attribution\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn order to perform the threat actor attribution, we did a correlation of the below data points.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e1.\u003c/strong\u003e C2 IP addresses\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e2.\u003c/strong\u003e Attacker-controlled Dropbox accounts’ registrant email addresses\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e3.\u003c/strong\u003e C2 domains’ registrant email addresses\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e4.\u003c/strong\u003e Passive DNS data\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e5.\u003c/strong\u003e Sender's email address in credential phishing attacks\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e6.\u003c/strong\u003e Sender's IP address in credential phishing attacks \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNote:\u003c/strong\u003e OSINT information related to the above data points was also used in correlation analysis.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e# Correlating different attacks to same threat actor\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs described in the network communication section later in the blog, the Stage-3 binary initially connects to an attacker-controlled Dropbox account to fetch a C2 domain which is used to perform further network communication.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn collaboration with Dropbox, we were able to discover the email addresses associated with the attacker-controlled Dropbox accounts used during this attack. One such email addresses was: \u003cstrong\u003epeterstewart0326@gmail[.]com\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis same email address was recently mentioned in Prevailion's \u003cu\u003eblog\u003c/u\u003e. It was linked to several domains which were used during Naver-themed phishing activity.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlso, according to this\u003ca href=\"https://blog.naver.com/PostView.naver?blogId=mrs_english\u0026amp;logNo=222289029970\"\u003e \u003cu\u003eblog\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e from 2021, this same email address was also used to send Naver-themed credential phishing attack emails to users in South Korea.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCorrelating the above data points, we can say with a high confidence level that the attack chains we have described in this blog are also related to the same threat actor.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e# Attribution to Lazarus APT\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAccording to the threat infrastructure mapping done in Prevailion \u003cu\u003eblog\u003c/u\u003e, the IP address \u003cstrong\u003e23.81.246[.]131\u003c/strong\u003e belongs to one of the critical nodes used by the threat actor during Naver-themed phishing activity. One of the domains linked to this IP address was \u003cstrong\u003enavercorpservice[.]com\u003c/strong\u003e. If we check the passive DNS data for this domain, we find two other IP address resolutions: \u003cstrong\u003e172.93.201[.]253\u003c/strong\u003e in November 2021 and \u003cstrong\u003e45.147.231[.]213\u003c/strong\u003e in September 2021.  \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe IP address \u003cstrong\u003e172.93.201[.]253\u003c/strong\u003e was recently used to host the domain - \u003cstrong\u003edisneycareers[.]net\u003c/strong\u003e which was attributed to Lazarus APT in Google TAG\u003ca href=\"https://blog.google/threat-analysis-group/countering-threats-north-korea/\"\u003e \u003cu\u003eblog\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFurther, what caught our attention was the IP address \u003cstrong\u003e45.147.231[.]213\u003c/strong\u003e. This IP address was earlier used by North Korea-based APT threat actor. Recently, we also had a new domain resolution alert for this IP address as part of our C2 infrastructure tracking. If we pivot on the passive DNS data for this IP address, we can see that the domain: \u003cstrong\u003ewww.devguardmap[.]org\u003c/strong\u003e was hosted on this IP address in Jan 2021 which was attributed to Lazarus APT as per this\u003ca href=\"https://twitter.com/ESETresearch/status/1458438155149922312\"\u003e \u003cu\u003etweet\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e from ESET and Google TAG\u003ca href=\"https://blog.google/threat-analysis-group/update-campaign-targeting-security-researchers/\"\u003e \u003cu\u003eblog\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCorrelating all the above data points, we reached the conclusion that the attack-chains we discovered are related to Lazarus threat actor. To the best of our knowledge, at the time of writing, this threat actor attribution has not been publicly documented yet.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eTechnical analysis\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor the purpose of technical analysis we will consider the attack chain starting with a Compiled HTML file having MD5 210db61d1b11c1d233fd8a0645946074.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e[+] Stage 1: Compiled HTML file\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe CHM file contains a malicious binary embedded inside it. At runtime, this will be dropped on the filesystem in the path: C:\\\\programdata\\\\chmtemp\\\\chmext.exe and executed.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe code responsible for extracting, dropping and executing the binary is present inside 1hh.html as shown below.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://cms.zscaler.com/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/Lazarus-SK-Naver/CHM.png\" data-entity-uuid=\"\" data-entity-type=\"\" alt=\"Compiled HTML\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eFigure 6: HTML code dropping and executing the binary\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e[+] Stage 2: Dropper\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe dropper on execution performs the following operations:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e1.\u003c/strong\u003e Detects sleep patching to identify controlled execution environment such as Sandbox execution\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e2.\u003c/strong\u003e Checks the name of all the running processes and terminates if it finds a process running with the name \u003cstrong\u003e\"v3l4sp.exe\"\u003c/strong\u003e. This process name corresponds to the security software developed by Ahnlab (a popular and frequently used security vendor in South Korea).\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e3.\u003c/strong\u003e Creates file in the path \u003cstrong\u003e\"C:\\ProgramData\\Intel\\IntelRST.exe\"\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e4.\u003c/strong\u003e XOR decodes the embedded PE from a hardcoded address\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e5. \u003c/strong\u003eWrites the decoded PE to the file created in Step-3\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e6.\u003c/strong\u003e Modifies PEB to masquerade itself as explorer.exe\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e7. \u003c/strong\u003eExecutes IntelRST.exe\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e8.\u003c/strong\u003e Creates RUN registry entry for persistence\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eValue:\u003c/strong\u003e IntelCUI\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eData: \u003c/strong\u003eC:\\ProgramData\\Intel\\IntelRST.exe\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e[+] Stage 3: Dropped binary\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe file IntelRST.exe dropped by the Stage-2 dropper is an ASpacked binary. On execution it performs the following operations:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e1. \u003c/strong\u003eSimilar to the dropper binary it tries to detect sleep patching to identify controlled execution environment\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e2. \u003c/strong\u003eCollects machine information and stores using the specified format which is later exfiltrated and used as machine identifier.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eString format:\u003c/strong\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e[decoded_string]_[username]_[volume_serial_number_post_8_bytes]\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003edecoded_string:\u003c/strong\u003e (encoded string) ^ (key) [encoded_string_byte_offset%keySize]\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eusername:\u003c/strong\u003e GetUserName()\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003evolume_serial _number:\u003c/strong\u003e Using DeviceIoControl with IOCTL_STORAGE_QUERY_PROPERTY (0x2d1400)\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e3.\u003c/strong\u003e Checks name of all the running processes and terminates if there is some process running with the name \"v3l4sp.exe\" or \"AYAgent.aye\" or \"IntelRST.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e4.\u003c/strong\u003e If running with administrator privileges then it executes a PowerShell command using cmd.exe to add WindowsDefender exclusion.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePowerShell command:\u003c/strong\u003e Powershell -Command Add-MpPreference -ExclusionPath \"C:\\ProgramData\\Intel\\IntelRST.exe\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e5.\u003c/strong\u003e  Finally it starts the network communication\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e[+] Network communication\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe network communication occurs in the following sequence:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e1. \u003c/strong\u003eSend a GET request to the URL \"https://dl.dropboxusercontent.com/s/k288s9tu2o53v41/zs_url.txt?dl=0\". \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e2. \u003c/strong\u003eQuery the file size and send another network request to read the file content.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNote:\u003c/strong\u003e The file content points to the C2 domain to be used for rest of the network communication.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e3.\u003c/strong\u003e Using the extracted C2 domain, send a POST request to the path \"/post.php\" and exfiltrate collected user information.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eExfiltrated user information format:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003euid={string_generated_in_Step-2_of_Stage-3_binary}\u0026amp;avtype=%d\u0026amp;majorv=%d\u0026amp;minorv=%d\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e4.\u003c/strong\u003e Finally send a GET request to the path \"/{decoded_string_from_step-2_of_Stage-3_binary}/{formated_string_from_step-2_of_Stage-3_binary}/fecommand.acm\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNote:\u003c/strong\u003e At the time of analysis we didn't get any active response from the C2 server for the above network request.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eZscaler Cloud Sandbox detection\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e# Document detection\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg
